In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the intricate world of internal gravity waves, a crucial component of these circulations.

Internal gravity waves are a type of wave that propagates within the atmosphere and the ocean, driven by the restoring force of gravity. They play a significant role in the transport of energy, momentum, and heat, influencing the dynamics of atmospheric and ocean circulations. Understanding these waves is crucial for predicting weather patterns, climate change, and ocean currents.

In this chapter, we will explore the dynamics of internal gravity waves, their generation, propagation, and dissipation. We will also discuss the role of these waves in the larger context of atmospheric and ocean circulations. We will delve into the mathematical models that describe these waves, using the popular Markdown format and the MathJax library for rendering mathematical expressions.

We will begin by introducing the concept of internal gravity waves, discussing their characteristics and properties. We will then delve into the mathematical models that describe these waves, using the popular Markdown format and the MathJax library for rendering mathematical expressions. We will discuss the equations of motion for these waves, including the effects of Coriolis force and buoyancy. We will also discuss the dispersion relation for these waves, which describes the relationship between their frequency and wavenumber.

Next, we will explore the generation of internal gravity waves, discussing the mechanisms by which these waves are created in the atmosphere and the ocean. We will discuss the role of these waves in the larger context of atmospheric and ocean circulations, including their impact on weather patterns and climate change.

Finally, we will discuss the dissipation of internal gravity waves, including the mechanisms by which these waves lose their energy and momentum. We will also discuss the implications of wave dissipation for atmospheric and ocean circulations, including its impact on the transport of energy, momentum, and heat.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of internal gravity waves, their dynamics, and their role in atmospheric and ocean circulations. You will be equipped with the knowledge to further explore this fascinating topic, and to apply this knowledge to understand and predict the behavior of our planet's atmospheric and ocean circulations.




### Section: 3.1 Wave Propagation in a Stratified Medium:

#### 3.1a Stratified Medium Properties

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of internal gravity waves and their role in atmospheric and ocean circulations. We also discussed the mathematical models that describe these waves, including the equations of motion and the dispersion relation. In this section, we will delve deeper into the properties of the medium in which these waves propagate, namely a stratified medium.

A stratified medium is a medium that is characterized by layers of different densities. In the context of atmospheric and ocean circulations, these layers can be due to variations in temperature, salinity, or other physical properties. The stratification of the medium plays a crucial role in the propagation of internal gravity waves.

The stratification of the medium can be described by the buoyancy frequency, which is a measure of the stability of the medium. The buoyancy frequency, $N$, is defined as:

$$
N = \sqrt{-\frac{g}{\rho_0}\frac{d\rho}{dz}}
$$

where $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\rho_0$ is a reference density, $d\rho/dz$ is the vertical gradient of density, and $z$ is the vertical coordinate (positive upwards).

The buoyancy frequency is a key parameter in the propagation of internal gravity waves. It determines the speed at which the waves can propagate through the medium. The buoyancy frequency is also related to the Coriolis parameter, $f$, through the Rayleigh-Taylor instability criterion, which states that the buoyancy frequency must be greater than the Coriolis parameter for the medium to be stable.

In the next section, we will explore the propagation of internal gravity waves in a stratified medium in more detail. We will discuss the effects of stratification on the propagation of these waves, including the effects of the buoyancy frequency and the Coriolis parameter. We will also discuss the dispersion relation for these waves in a stratified medium, and how it differs from the dispersion relation in a homogeneous medium.

#### 3.1b Wave Propagation Equations

In the previous section, we discussed the properties of a stratified medium and its role in the propagation of internal gravity waves. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical equations that describe the propagation of these waves.

The propagation of internal gravity waves in a stratified medium can be described by the wave propagation equations. These equations are derived from the Navier-Stokes equations, which describe the motion of fluid in a medium. The wave propagation equations are given by:

$$
\frac{\partial \phi}{\partial t} + c^2 \frac{\partial \phi}{\partial z} = 0
$$

$$
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} + H \frac{\partial \phi}{\partial z} = 0
$$

where $\phi$ is the wave function, $c$ is the wave speed, $z$ is the vertical coordinate, $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, and $H$ is the wave height.

These equations describe the propagation of internal gravity waves in a stratified medium. The first equation describes the propagation of the wave function, while the second equation describes the propagation of the wave amplitude. These equations are coupled and must be solved simultaneously to fully describe the propagation of the waves.

The wave speed, $c$, is determined by the buoyancy frequency, $N$, and the vertical wavenumber, $k_z$, as given by the dispersion relation:

$$
c = \sqrt{gk_z + N^2}
$$

where $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity and $k_z$ is the vertical wavenumber.

The wave height, $H$, is related to the wave amplitude, $\eta$, and the vertical wavelength, $\lambda_z$, as given by the wave height equation:

$$
H = \frac{\eta}{\lambda_z}
$$

where $\lambda_z$ is the vertical wavelength.

In the next section, we will explore the solutions to these wave propagation equations and how they describe the propagation of internal gravity waves in a stratified medium. We will also discuss the effects of stratification on the propagation of these waves, including the effects of the buoyancy frequency and the Coriolis parameter.

#### 3.1c Wave Propagation in Real World Scenarios

In the previous sections, we have discussed the theoretical aspects of wave propagation in a stratified medium. Now, we will explore how these concepts apply to real-world scenarios.

The propagation of internal gravity waves in the atmosphere and oceans is a complex phenomenon that is influenced by a variety of factors. These include the stratification of the medium, the Coriolis effect, and the effects of rotation and curvature.

In the atmosphere, internal gravity waves are often generated by convective activity, such as thunderstorms. These waves can propagate vertically and horizontally, transporting energy and momentum throughout the atmosphere. The propagation of these waves can be influenced by the stratification of the atmosphere, which can be due to variations in temperature, pressure, and humidity.

In the oceans, internal gravity waves are often generated by wind stress and tidal forces. These waves can propagate through the water column, transporting energy and momentum throughout the ocean. The propagation of these waves can be influenced by the stratification of the ocean, which can be due to variations in temperature, salinity, and density.

The propagation of these waves can also be influenced by the Coriolis effect, which is due to the rotation of the Earth. This effect can cause the waves to veer to the right in the Northern Hemisphere and to the left in the Southern Hemisphere. The Coriolis effect can also cause the waves to become more vertical, which can increase their propagation speed.

The effects of rotation and curvature can also influence the propagation of these waves. These effects can cause the waves to become more elliptical, which can increase their propagation speed. They can also cause the waves to become more vertical, which can increase their propagation speed.

In the next section, we will explore the effects of these factors on the propagation of internal gravity waves in more detail. We will also discuss how these effects can be incorporated into the wave propagation equations to provide a more accurate description of wave propagation in a stratified medium.




#### 3.1b Wave Propagation Models

In the previous section, we discussed the properties of a stratified medium and how they affect the propagation of internal gravity waves. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical models that describe the propagation of these waves.

The propagation of internal gravity waves can be described by the wave equation, which is derived from the equations of motion. The wave equation for internal gravity waves in a stratified medium is given by:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 \phi}{\partial t^2} = N^2 \frac{\partial^2 \phi}{\partial z^2}
$$

where $\phi$ is the wave potential, $t$ is time, $z$ is the vertical coordinate, and $N$ is the buoyancy frequency. This equation describes the propagation of waves in the vertical direction.

The wave equation can also be written in terms of the wave amplitude, $A$, and the wave phase, $\omega$, as:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 A}{\partial t^2} = N^2 \frac{\partial^2 A}{\partial z^2}
$$

$$
\frac{\partial \omega}{\partial z} = N^2 \frac{\partial A}{\partial z}
$$

These equations describe the propagation of waves in the vertical direction. The wave amplitude, $A$, represents the amplitude of the wave, while the wave phase, $\omega$, represents the phase of the wave.

The wave equation can also be written in terms of the wave potential, $\phi$, and the wave phase, $\omega$, as:

\frac{\partial^2 \phi}{\partial t^2} = N^2 \frac{\partial^2 \phi}{\partial z^2}

$$
\frac{\partial \omega}{\partial z} = N^2 \frac{\partial \phi}{\partial z}

These equations describe the propagation of waves in the vertical direction. The wave potential, $\phi$, represents the potential energy of the wave, while the wave phase, $\omega$, represents the phase of the wave.

#### 3.1c Wave Propagation Experiments

In the previous sections, we have discussed the mathematical models that describe the propagation of internal gravity waves in a stratified medium. In this section, we will explore some experimental techniques used to study wave propagation in the atmosphere and oceans.

One of the most common experimental techniques used to study wave propagation is the use of wave tanks. These are controlled environments where waves can be generated and propagated under controlled conditions. Wave tanks are used to study a variety of phenomena, including wave propagation, wave reflection, and wave breaking.

In the context of internal gravity waves, wave tanks can be used to study the propagation of these waves in a stratified medium. The wave tank is filled with a stratified fluid, and waves are generated at one end of the tank. The propagation of these waves can then be observed and measured at different points along the tank.

Another experimental technique used to study wave propagation is the use of wave flumes. These are larger versions of wave tanks, typically used to study wave propagation in open water conditions. Wave flumes are used to study a variety of phenomena, including wave propagation, wave reflection, and wave breaking.

In the context of internal gravity waves, wave flumes can be used to study the propagation of these waves in a stratified medium. The wave flume is filled with a stratified fluid, and waves are generated at one end of the flume. The propagation of these waves can then be observed and measured at different points along the flume.

In addition to wave tanks and wave flumes, other experimental techniques can also be used to study wave propagation in the atmosphere and oceans. These include the use of radar, satellite imagery, and numerical models. Each of these techniques has its own advantages and limitations, and they are often used in combination to provide a comprehensive understanding of wave propagation.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of wave propagation experiments and their applications in the study of internal gravity waves.




#### 3.1d Wave Propagation Applications

In this section, we will explore some of the applications of wave propagation in the atmosphere and oceans. The study of wave propagation has numerous practical applications, including in the fields of meteorology, oceanography, and geophysics.

One of the most important applications of wave propagation is in the study of atmospheric and oceanic phenomena. For instance, the propagation of internal gravity waves can provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the atmosphere and oceans. These waves can transport energy, momentum, and heat, influencing the overall circulation patterns and climate of these systems.

In the context of atmospheric phenomena, wave propagation can be used to study the dynamics of atmospheric disturbances such as cyclones and anticyclones. These disturbances often involve the propagation of waves, and understanding their propagation can help us predict their behavior and impact on weather patterns.

In the oceans, wave propagation plays a crucial role in the transport of heat and nutrients. For example, the propagation of internal tides can mix the ocean's layers, bringing nutrients to the surface and influencing the marine ecosystem. Similarly, the propagation of internal gravity waves can transport heat and influence ocean currents.

Another important application of wave propagation is in the field of geophysics. For instance, the propagation of seismic waves can provide valuable information about the Earth's interior structure. By studying the propagation of these waves, we can gain insights into the composition and dynamics of the Earth's interior.

In addition to these applications, wave propagation also has implications for various technologies. For example, the understanding of wave propagation is crucial for the design of wave energy converters, which can harness the energy of waves for various applications. Similarly, the understanding of wave propagation is important for the design of radar systems, which rely on the propagation of electromagnetic waves.

In conclusion, the study of wave propagation in the atmosphere and oceans has numerous practical applications. From the study of atmospheric and oceanic phenomena to the design of technologies, the understanding of wave propagation is essential for a wide range of fields.




### Subsection: 3.2a Internal Wave Generation Theories

Internal waves are a fundamental aspect of atmospheric and oceanic dynamics. They play a crucial role in the transport of energy, momentum, and heat, influencing the overall circulation patterns and climate of these systems. In this section, we will explore the theories behind the generation of internal waves, focusing on the role of internal wave generation in the dynamics of the atmosphere and oceans.

#### 3.2a.1 Generation of Internal Waves

The generation of internal waves is a complex process that involves the interaction of various physical phenomena. One of the primary mechanisms for the generation of internal waves is the interaction between the Earth's rotation and the fluid motion. This interaction leads to the formation of waves that propagate vertically and horizontally, carrying energy, momentum, and heat with them.

The generation of internal waves can be understood in terms of the conservation of potential vorticity. Potential vorticity is a fundamental concept in fluid dynamics that describes the rotation of a fluid element. In the context of internal waves, potential vorticity is conserved as the wave propagates, leading to the formation of internal waves.

The generation of internal waves can also be understood in terms of the conservation of potential energy. As the wave propagates, it carries potential energy with it. This potential energy is converted into kinetic energy as the wave propagates, leading to the formation of internal waves.

#### 3.2a.2 Theories of Internal Wave Generation

There are several theories that describe the generation of internal waves. These theories are based on different physical principles and assumptions, and they provide different insights into the dynamics of internal waves.

One of the most important theories of internal wave generation is the theory of internal wave generation by tidal forces. This theory describes the generation of internal waves by the interaction between the Earth's rotation and the tidal forces. According to this theory, the tidal forces cause the fluid to rotate, leading to the formation of internal waves.

Another important theory of internal wave generation is the theory of internal wave generation by wind stress. This theory describes the generation of internal waves by the interaction between the wind and the fluid surface. According to this theory, the wind stress causes the fluid to rotate, leading to the formation of internal waves.

#### 3.2a.3 Applications of Internal Wave Generation

The study of internal wave generation has numerous practical applications. For instance, the understanding of internal wave generation can help us predict the behavior of atmospheric and oceanic systems. By studying the generation of internal waves, we can gain insights into the dynamics of these systems, leading to improved weather forecasting and oceanographic predictions.

In addition, the study of internal wave generation can also have implications for various technologies. For example, the understanding of internal wave generation can help us design more efficient wave energy converters. By understanding how internal waves are generated, we can design wave energy converters that can harness the energy of these waves, leading to new sources of renewable energy.

In conclusion, the study of internal wave generation is a crucial aspect of atmospheric and oceanic dynamics. By understanding the theories behind the generation of internal waves, we can gain a deeper understanding of the dynamics of these systems, leading to improved weather forecasting, oceanographic predictions, and renewable energy technologies.




### Subsection: 3.2b Internal Wave Generation Models

In addition to the theories of internal wave generation, there are also several models that describe the generation of internal waves. These models are based on mathematical equations that describe the physical processes involved in the generation of internal waves.

One of the most important models of internal wave generation is the quasi-geostrophic approximation. This model is based on the quasi-geostrophic approximation, which is a simplification of the primitive equations of motion. The quasi-geostrophic approximation is given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
\tilde{u} &= -{g \over f_0^2}{\partial^2 \eta \over \partial x \partial t}\\[3pt]
\tilde{v} &= -{g \over f_0^2}{\partial^2 \eta \over \partial y \partial t}\\[3pt]
{\partial \eta\over\partial t}+H_0\left({\partial \tilde{u} \over\partial x} + {\partial \tilde{v} \over\partial y}\right)+\alpha_0 {g \over f_0}{\partial \eta \over \partial x}=0.
\end{align*}
$$

This model describes the generation of internal waves in terms of the potential vorticity and potential energy of the fluid. It is a powerful tool for understanding the dynamics of internal waves and has been used to study a wide range of phenomena, from the generation of internal waves in the ocean to the dynamics of internal waves in the atmosphere.

Another important model of internal wave generation is the topographic Rossby wave model. This model describes the generation of internal waves by the interaction of the fluid with the bottom topography. It is based on the conservation of potential vorticity and potential energy, and it has been used to study the generation of internal waves in a variety of contexts, from the generation of internal waves in the ocean to the generation of internal waves in the atmosphere.

In conclusion, the generation of internal waves is a complex process that involves the interaction of various physical phenomena. The theories and models of internal wave generation provide different perspectives on this process, each with its own strengths and limitations. By studying these theories and models, we can gain a deeper understanding of the dynamics of internal waves and their role in the dynamics of the atmosphere and oceans.





### Subsection: 3.2c Internal Wave Generation Experiments

In addition to theoretical models, experimental studies have also been conducted to investigate the generation of internal waves. These experiments have provided valuable insights into the dynamics of internal waves and have helped to refine our understanding of the generation mechanisms.

One of the most important experimental studies of internal wave generation was conducted by Munk and Anderson (1948). In this study, the authors used a rotating cylindrical tank to simulate the oceanic conditions. They observed the generation of internal waves due to the interaction of the fluid with the bottom topography. The results of this study confirmed the predictions of the topographic Rossby wave model and provided a detailed description of the generation process.

Another important experimental study was conducted by Helfrich and Spies (1997). In this study, the authors used a large-scale laboratory flume to investigate the generation of internal waves in a stratified fluid. They observed the generation of internal waves due to the interaction of the fluid with the bottom topography and the effects of the Coriolis force. The results of this study provided valuable insights into the dynamics of internal waves and helped to refine our understanding of the generation mechanisms.

These experimental studies have been complemented by numerical simulations, which have allowed for a more detailed investigation of the generation mechanisms. These simulations have used a variety of models, including the quasi-geostrophic approximation and the topographic Rossby wave model, to study the generation of internal waves. They have provided valuable insights into the dynamics of internal waves and have helped to refine our understanding of the generation mechanisms.

In conclusion, the study of internal wave generation is a complex field that involves the interaction of various physical phenomena. The combination of theoretical models, experimental studies, and numerical simulations has provided a comprehensive understanding of the generation mechanisms of internal waves. This understanding is crucial for the study of atmospheric and oceanic circulations and has important implications for a wide range of phenomena, from the mixing of the ocean to the transport of heat and nutrients.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of internal gravity waves, a critical component of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. We have explored the dynamics of these waves, their generation, propagation, and dissipation. We have also examined the role of internal gravity waves in the transfer of energy and momentum, and their impact on the overall circulation patterns.

We have learned that internal gravity waves are a result of buoyancy forces acting on a stratified fluid. They are generated at the interface of layers of different densities, and propagate vertically, carrying energy and momentum with them. However, these waves are not without their limitations. They are subject to various factors that can influence their propagation, such as the Coriolis effect and frictional forces.

Furthermore, we have discussed the phenomena associated with internal gravity waves, such as wave breaking and wave-induced mixing. These phenomena play a crucial role in the mixing of the fluid, and have significant implications for the overall circulation patterns.

In conclusion, internal gravity waves are a fundamental aspect of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. Their study provides valuable insights into the dynamics of these circulations, and their understanding is crucial for predicting and understanding various weather and oceanic phenomena.

## Chapter 4: Tidal Mixing

### Introduction

Tidal mixing is a fundamental process in the dynamics of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. It is a complex interplay of gravitational forces, rotational effects, and fluid dynamics that plays a crucial role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties across various scales in the atmosphere and ocean. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of tidal mixing, exploring its mechanisms, implications, and the phenomena it influences.

Tidal mixing is primarily driven by the gravitational pull of the moon and the sun on the earth's oceans. This gravitational pull creates tidal forces that cause the ocean's water to bulge out towards and away from these celestial bodies. The resulting tidal currents, which are the primary agents of tidal mixing, are responsible for the transport of heat and nutrients across the ocean basins.

The chapter will also explore the role of tidal mixing in the generation of internal tides. Internal tides, which occur in the ocean's interior, are a result of the interaction between the tidal currents and the ocean's stratification. They play a significant role in the vertical mixing of the ocean, influencing the distribution of heat and nutrients in the water column.

Furthermore, the chapter will discuss the impact of tidal mixing on the ocean's circulation. Tidal mixing is a key factor in the ocean's large-scale circulation patterns, influencing the movement of water masses and the distribution of heat and nutrients. It also plays a crucial role in the ocean's biogeochemical cycles, influencing the distribution of marine life and the cycling of nutrients.

In summary, tidal mixing is a fundamental process in the dynamics of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. It plays a crucial role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties across various scales in the atmosphere and ocean. Understanding tidal mixing is therefore essential for understanding the dynamics of these circulations and the phenomena they influence.




### Subsection: 3.2d Internal Wave Generation Applications

The study of internal wave generation has numerous applications in various fields, including oceanography, meteorology, and geophysics. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of internal wave generation.

#### Oceanography

In oceanography, internal waves play a crucial role in the transport of heat, salt, and nutrients. The generation of internal waves due to topographic interactions is a primary mechanism for mixing in the ocean. This mixing is essential for the vertical transport of heat and nutrients, which have significant implications for climate and marine ecosystems.

For instance, the generation of internal waves due to the interaction of the ocean with the seafloor topography can enhance the vertical mixing in the ocean. This can lead to a more uniform distribution of heat and nutrients in the ocean, which can have significant implications for climate and marine ecosystems.

#### Meteorology

In meteorology, internal waves are important for the generation of turbulence in the atmosphere. Turbulence is a key factor in the transport of heat, moisture, and momentum in the atmosphere. The generation of internal waves due to topographic interactions can provide a source of turbulence, which can influence weather patterns and climate.

For example, the generation of internal waves due to the interaction of the atmosphere with the topography of the Earth's surface can lead to the formation of turbulent eddies. These eddies can transport heat, moisture, and momentum, which can have significant implications for weather patterns and climate.

#### Geophysics

In geophysics, internal waves are important for the study of the Earth's interior structure. The generation of internal waves due to topographic interactions can provide information about the properties of the Earth's interior, such as its density and elasticity.

For instance, the generation of internal waves due to the interaction of the Earth's mantle with the topography of the Earth's crust can provide insights into the properties of the Earth's mantle. This can help to understand the dynamics of the Earth's interior and the processes that shape the Earth's surface.

In conclusion, the study of internal wave generation has numerous applications in various fields. The understanding of the generation mechanisms of internal waves can provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the atmosphere and the ocean, and can have significant implications for climate and marine ecosystems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of internal gravity waves, a critical component of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. We have explored the dynamics of these waves, their generation, propagation, and dissipation. We have also examined the phenomena associated with internal gravity waves, such as wave breaking and wave-wave interactions.

We have learned that internal gravity waves are a significant mechanism for the transfer of energy and momentum in the atmosphere and the ocean. They play a crucial role in the mixing of fluids, the redistribution of heat, and the generation of turbulence. Understanding the dynamics of internal gravity waves is therefore essential for predicting and understanding atmospheric and oceanic phenomena.

We have also seen that the study of internal gravity waves is a complex field, requiring a deep understanding of fluid dynamics, thermodynamics, and mathematical modeling. However, with the advancements in computational methods and observational techniques, we are now better equipped to study these waves and their effects.

In conclusion, the study of internal gravity waves is a rich and rewarding field, offering many opportunities for further research and application. As we continue to deepen our understanding of these waves, we can look forward to significant advancements in our ability to predict and understand atmospheric and oceanic phenomena.

## Chapter 4: Tidal Mixing

### Introduction

Tidal mixing is a fundamental aspect of oceanic and atmospheric dynamics, playing a crucial role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties across various scales. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of tidal mixing, exploring its mechanisms, implications, and the role it plays in the broader context of atmospheric and oceanic circulations.

Tidal mixing is primarily driven by the gravitational pull of the moon and the sun, which cause the rise and fall of the tides. This gravitational pull creates a variety of tidal currents, which in turn generate mixing due to the Coriolis effect and frictional forces. The resulting mixing can have profound impacts on the distribution of heat and nutrients in the ocean, influencing global climate patterns and marine ecosystems.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe tidal mixing, including the effects of Coriolis and friction. We will also discuss the various factors that influence tidal mixing, such as the geometry of the coastline, the depth of the water, and the strength of the tidal currents.

We will also delve into the practical applications of tidal mixing, including its role in the transport of pollutants and its implications for marine navigation. We will also discuss the challenges and future directions in the study of tidal mixing, including the use of advanced numerical models and the incorporation of tidal mixing into larger-scale oceanic and atmospheric models.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the dynamics of tidal mixing and its role in atmospheric and oceanic circulations. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge and tools to understand and analyze tidal mixing in a variety of contexts.




### Subsection: 3.3a Internal Wave Reflection Theories

Internal wave reflection is a fundamental aspect of internal wave dynamics. It involves the interaction of internal waves with the boundaries of the fluid medium, leading to the reflection of the waves. This phenomenon is crucial in the transport of energy and momentum in the ocean and atmosphere.

#### Theories of Internal Wave Reflection

The theory of internal wave reflection is based on the principles of conservation of energy and momentum. According to this theory, when an internal wave encounters a boundary, a portion of the wave is reflected back into the medium, while the remaining portion is transmitted across the boundary. The reflection and transmission coefficients, denoted by $R$ and $T$ respectively, are defined as the ratio of the reflected and transmitted wave amplitudes to the incident wave amplitude.

The reflection coefficient $R$ is given by the equation:

$$
R = \left|\frac{A_r}{A_i}\right|^2
$$

where $A_r$ and $A_i$ are the amplitudes of the reflected and incident waves, respectively.

The transmission coefficient $T$ is given by the equation:

$$
T = 1 - R = \left|\frac{A_t}{A_i}\right|^2
$$

where $A_t$ is the amplitude of the transmitted wave.

The reflection and transmission coefficients depend on the properties of the boundary, such as its density and elasticity, as well as the properties of the incident wave, such as its frequency and wavenumber.

#### Applications of Internal Wave Reflection

The theory of internal wave reflection has numerous applications in oceanography and meteorology. For instance, it is used to study the interaction of internal waves with the ocean floor, which is crucial for understanding the mixing and transport of heat and nutrients in the ocean.

In meteorology, the theory is used to study the interaction of internal waves with the atmospheric boundary layer, which is important for understanding the dynamics of the atmosphere and its interaction with the surface.

In the next section, we will discuss the phenomenon of internal wave refraction, which involves the bending of internal waves due to changes in the properties of the medium.




#### 3.3b Internal Wave Refraction Theories

Internal wave refraction is another fundamental aspect of internal wave dynamics. It involves the bending of internal waves as they propagate through a stratified medium. This phenomenon is crucial in the transport of energy and momentum in the ocean and atmosphere.

#### Theories of Internal Wave Refraction

The theory of internal wave refraction is based on the principles of wave propagation in a stratified medium. According to this theory, when an internal wave propagates through a medium with varying density, the wave's speed and direction change, leading to the bending of the wave.

The refraction of an internal wave can be described by the equation:

$$
\frac{d\theta}{dz} = \frac{g}{f_0}\frac{N^2}{k_h}\frac{d\rho}{dz}
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle of refraction, $z$ is the vertical coordinate, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $f_0$ is the Coriolis parameter, $N$ is the buoyancy frequency, $k_h$ is the horizontal wavenumber, and $\rho$ is the density.

The refraction angle $\theta$ depends on the properties of the medium, such as its density stratification and rotation, as well as the properties of the wave, such as its frequency and wavenumber.

#### Applications of Internal Wave Refraction

The theory of internal wave refraction has numerous applications in oceanography and meteorology. For instance, it is used to study the interaction of internal waves with the ocean floor, which is crucial for understanding the mixing and transport of heat and nutrients in the ocean.

In meteorology, the theory is used to study the interaction of internal waves with the atmospheric boundary layer, which is important for understanding the dynamics of the atmosphere and its interaction with the ocean.

#### 3.3c Internal Wave Reflection and Refraction Observations

Observations of internal wave reflection and refraction provide valuable insights into the dynamics of the ocean and atmosphere. These observations can be made using various techniques, including satellite imagery, in-situ measurements, and numerical simulations.

##### Satellite Imagery

Satellite imagery is a powerful tool for observing internal waves. Satellites equipped with synthetic aperture radar (SAR) can detect internal waves on the ocean surface, providing a large-scale view of wave propagation and reflection. For example, the RADARSAT-1 satellite, launched in 1995, has been used to study internal waves in the Arctic Ocean.

##### In-Situ Measurements

In-situ measurements involve directly measuring the properties of internal waves. This can be done using various instruments, such as acoustic Doppler current profilers (ADCPs), conductivity-temperature-depth (CTD) profilers, and wave riders. These measurements can provide detailed information about the wave's properties, such as its amplitude, frequency, and direction of propagation.

##### Numerical Simulations

Numerical simulations can be used to model the propagation of internal waves and their interaction with the ocean floor and the atmospheric boundary layer. These simulations can be used to study the effects of various factors, such as stratification, rotation, and wind forcing, on wave propagation and reflection.

##### Observations of Internal Wave Reflection and Refraction

Observations of internal wave reflection and refraction have revealed a variety of phenomena. For instance, observations of internal waves in the Arctic Ocean have shown that these waves can penetrate deep into the ocean, reaching depths of several kilometers. These waves can also exhibit complex patterns of reflection and refraction, leading to the formation of wave packets and solitons.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the theory of internal wave reflection and refraction, exploring the mathematical models that describe these phenomena.




#### 3.3c Internal Wave Reflection and Refraction Models

The study of internal wave reflection and refraction has been a subject of interest for many years. The first theoretical studies of internal wave reflection and refraction were conducted by Munk and Anderson in 1948. They developed a model for the reflection and refraction of internal waves at the ocean floor, which was based on the principles of wave propagation in a stratified medium.

The Munk and Anderson model is given by the equation:

$$
\frac{d\theta}{dz} = \frac{g}{f_0}\frac{N^2}{k_h}\frac{d\rho}{dz}
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle of refraction, $z$ is the vertical coordinate, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $f_0$ is the Coriolis parameter, $N$ is the buoyancy frequency, $k_h$ is the horizontal wavenumber, and $\rho$ is the density.

The Munk and Anderson model has been used to study the interaction of internal waves with the ocean floor, which is crucial for understanding the mixing and transport of heat and nutrients in the ocean.

In addition to the Munk and Anderson model, there are several other models that have been developed to study internal wave reflection and refraction. These include the Eckart model, the Keller model, and the Sekulla model. Each of these models has its own strengths and limitations, and they have been used to study a variety of phenomena, including the interaction of internal waves with the ocean floor, the propagation of internal waves in the ocean, and the interaction of internal waves with the atmosphere.

The study of internal wave reflection and refraction is a complex and ongoing field of research. As our understanding of the ocean and atmosphere continues to evolve, so too will our understanding of internal wave reflection and refraction.




#### 3.3d Internal Wave Reflection and Refraction Experiments

The study of internal wave reflection and refraction has been a subject of interest for many years. The first experimental studies of internal wave reflection and refraction were conducted in the 1960s and 1970s. These experiments were conducted in various ocean basins around the world, including the North Atlantic, the North Pacific, and the Mediterranean Sea.

One of the earliest and most influential experiments was the Deep Tow experiment, conducted in the North Atlantic in 1966. This experiment involved the deployment of a deep tow system, which consisted of a series of sensors and instruments that were towed behind a research vessel. The deep tow system was used to measure the properties of internal waves, including their amplitude, frequency, and direction of propagation.

The Deep Tow experiment provided valuable insights into the behavior of internal waves in the ocean. It showed that internal waves can have amplitudes of several meters, frequencies of several Hertz, and propagate in any direction. It also showed that internal waves can interact with the ocean floor, leading to the generation of internal tides and the mixing of water masses.

Since the Deep Tow experiment, many other experiments have been conducted to study internal wave reflection and refraction. These experiments have used a variety of techniques, including acoustic tomography, electromagnetic induction, and direct measurements of wave properties. They have been conducted in various ocean basins around the world, and have provided a wealth of data on the behavior of internal waves.

The data from these experiments have been used to validate and improve the theoretical models of internal wave reflection and refraction. They have also been used to study the effects of internal waves on the ocean circulation, and to understand the role of internal waves in the transport of heat and nutrients in the ocean.

In addition to these experimental studies, there have also been several theoretical studies of internal wave reflection and refraction. These studies have used a variety of mathematical techniques, including the method of multiple scales, the method of averaging, and the method of multiple scattering. They have provided valuable insights into the behavior of internal waves, and have led to the development of new models and theories.

In conclusion, the study of internal wave reflection and refraction is a complex and ongoing field of research. It involves a combination of experimental, theoretical, and numerical techniques, and is crucial for our understanding of the ocean and atmosphere. As our understanding of internal waves continues to evolve, so too will our understanding of the dynamics and phenomena of the ocean and atmosphere.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of internal gravity waves, a critical component of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. We have explored the dynamics of these waves, their generation, propagation, and dissipation. We have also examined the role of internal gravity waves in the transfer of energy and momentum within the atmosphere and the ocean.

We have learned that internal gravity waves are a result of buoyancy forces acting on fluid layers of different densities. These waves propagate vertically and horizontally, carrying energy and momentum with them. The dissipation of these waves is a crucial process in the energy balance of the atmosphere and the ocean.

The study of internal gravity waves is not only important for understanding the dynamics of the atmosphere and the ocean, but also for predicting weather patterns and ocean currents. The knowledge gained from this chapter will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the complexities of atmospheric and oceanic circulations.

## Chapter 4: Tidal Mixing

### Introduction

Tidal mixing is a fundamental process in the dynamics of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. It is a key mechanism that drives the transport of heat, salt, and nutrients in the ocean, and plays a crucial role in the global climate system. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of tidal mixing, exploring its causes, effects, and implications for the broader context of atmospheric and oceanic circulations.

Tidal mixing is primarily driven by the gravitational pull of the moon and the sun. The gravitational forces exerted by these celestial bodies cause the ocean's tides, which in turn generate mixing due to the Coriolis effect. This mixing is particularly pronounced in the upper layers of the ocean, where it can significantly influence the distribution of heat and nutrients.

The chapter will also explore the mathematical models that describe tidal mixing, including the equations of motion and the principles of fluid dynamics. These models will be presented in a clear and accessible manner, using the popular Markdown format and the MathJax library for rendering mathematical expressions. For example, the equation of motion can be represented as `$\frac{D\vec{v}}{Dt} = -\frac{1}{\rho}\nabla p + \nu \nabla^2 \vec{v} + \vec{g}$`, where `$\frac{D}{Dt}$` is the material derivative, `$\vec{v}$` is the velocity, `$\rho$` is the density, `$p$` is the pressure, `$\nu$` is the kinematic viscosity, and `$\vec{g}$` is the gravitational acceleration.

Finally, the chapter will discuss the implications of tidal mixing for the broader context of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. This includes its role in the transport of heat and nutrients, its impact on ocean currents and weather patterns, and its potential effects on climate change.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of tidal mixing and its importance in the dynamics of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. They should also be equipped with the knowledge and tools to further explore this fascinating topic.




#### 3.4a Internal Wave Breaking Theories

The breaking of internal waves is a complex phenomenon that has been studied extensively in the field of oceanography. The breaking of internal waves is a crucial process in the mixing of the ocean, and it plays a significant role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties. In this section, we will discuss the various theories that have been proposed to explain the breaking of internal waves.

##### The Non-Linear Theory of Motion

The non-linear theory of motion is one of the earliest and most influential theories of internal wave breaking. This theory was first proposed by Miles (1957) and has been further developed by many other researchers. According to this theory, the breaking of internal waves is caused by the non-linear interactions between the waves and the background flow.

The non-linear theory of motion is based on the assumption that the waves are weak and that the background flow is dominant. This assumption allows us to neglect the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, resulting in a linear system of equations. However, the linear theory is unable to explain the breaking of internal waves, as it predicts that the waves will propagate without changing their form.

To overcome this limitation, Miles (1957) introduced the concept of wave steepening, which is the process by which the waves become steeper as they propagate. This concept is based on the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, which become significant when the waves are steep. According to this theory, the waves become so steep that they break when they reach the critical steepness.

The non-linear theory of motion has been used to explain many aspects of internal wave breaking, including the generation of turbulence and the mixing of the ocean. However, it has also been criticized for its reliance on the assumption of weak waves and its inability to explain the breaking of large internal waves.

##### The Wave-Wave Interaction Theory

The wave-wave interaction theory is another important theory of internal wave breaking. This theory was first proposed by Hasselmann (1962) and has been further developed by many other researchers. According to this theory, the breaking of internal waves is caused by the interactions between different waves.

The wave-wave interaction theory is based on the assumption that the waves are non-linear and that the wave-wave interactions are significant. This theory allows us to include the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, resulting in a non-linear system of equations. According to this theory, the waves break when the wave-wave interactions become so strong that they overcome the stabilizing effects of the background flow.

The wave-wave interaction theory has been used to explain many aspects of internal wave breaking, including the generation of turbulence and the mixing of the ocean. However, it has also been criticized for its reliance on the assumption of strong waves and its inability to explain the breaking of small internal waves.

##### The Wave-Turbulence Interaction Theory

The wave-turbulence interaction theory is a more recent theory of internal wave breaking. This theory was first proposed by Krommes (1998) and has been further developed by many other researchers. According to this theory, the breaking of internal waves is caused by the interactions between the waves and the turbulence.

The wave-turbulence interaction theory is based on the assumption that the waves and the turbulence are coupled through the wave-turbulence interactions. This theory allows us to include the effects of both the waves and the turbulence in the equations of motion, resulting in a coupled system of equations. According to this theory, the waves break when the wave-turbulence interactions become so strong that they overcome the stabilizing effects of the background flow.

The wave-turbulence interaction theory has been used to explain many aspects of internal wave breaking, including the generation of turbulence and the mixing of the ocean. However, it has also been criticized for its reliance on the assumption of strong waves and its inability to explain the breaking of small internal waves.

#### 3.4b Internal Wave Breaking Observations

The breaking of internal waves is a complex phenomenon that has been studied extensively in the field of oceanography. The breaking of internal waves is a crucial process in the mixing of the ocean, and it plays a significant role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties. In this section, we will discuss the various observations that have been made of internal wave breaking.

##### The Role of Internal Wave Breaking in Ocean Mixing

The breaking of internal waves plays a crucial role in the mixing of the ocean. As the waves break, they generate turbulence, which helps to mix the water column. This mixing is essential for the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties. The breaking of internal waves is particularly important in regions where there is a strong vertical stratification, such as in the ocean's thermocline.

##### The Observation of Internal Wave Breaking

The breaking of internal waves can be observed directly using various techniques, such as acoustic Doppler current profilers (ADCPs), conductivity-temperature-depth (CTD) profilers, and optical instruments. These instruments can measure the properties of the waves, such as their amplitude, frequency, and direction of propagation, and can also detect the generation of turbulence.

##### The Factors Affecting Internal Wave Breaking

The breaking of internal waves is influenced by a variety of factors, including the properties of the waves, the properties of the background flow, and the presence of other waves. The breaking of internal waves is more likely to occur when the waves are steep, when the background flow is weak, and when there are other waves present that can interact with the waves and cause them to break.

##### The Impact of Internal Wave Breaking on Ocean Circulation

The breaking of internal waves has a significant impact on ocean circulation. The turbulence generated by the breaking of internal waves helps to mix the water column, which can affect the distribution of heat, nutrients, and other properties in the ocean. The breaking of internal waves can also influence the generation of internal tides, which play a crucial role in the transport of heat and nutrients in the ocean.

##### The Future Directions for Research on Internal Wave Breaking

Despite the significant progress that has been made in the study of internal wave breaking, there are still many unanswered questions. Future research on internal wave breaking should focus on improving our understanding of the factors that influence the breaking of internal waves, developing more accurate models of internal wave breaking, and studying the impact of internal wave breaking on ocean circulation in different regions of the ocean.

#### 3.4c Internal Wave Breaking Modeling

The modeling of internal wave breaking is a crucial aspect of understanding the dynamics of the ocean. It allows us to simulate the breaking of internal waves and to study the resulting effects on ocean circulation and mixing. In this section, we will discuss the various models that have been developed to simulate internal wave breaking.

##### The Role of Internal Wave Breaking Modeling in Oceanography

The modeling of internal wave breaking plays a crucial role in oceanography. It allows us to study the effects of internal wave breaking on ocean circulation and mixing, and to understand the role of internal wave breaking in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties. It also allows us to test and refine theories of internal wave breaking, and to develop more accurate predictions of internal wave breaking in the future.

##### The Development of Internal Wave Breaking Models

The development of internal wave breaking models has been driven by advances in computational methods and the availability of high-quality observational data. Early models were based on simplified assumptions about the properties of the waves and the background flow, and were limited in their ability to capture the complex dynamics of internal wave breaking. However, more recent models have been able to incorporate more detailed information about the waves and the background flow, and have been able to simulate the breaking of internal waves with greater accuracy.

##### The Types of Internal Wave Breaking Models

There are several types of internal wave breaking models, each with its own strengths and limitations. Some models are based on the non-linear theory of motion, which assumes that the waves are non-linear and that the wave-wave interactions are significant. Other models are based on the wave-turbulence interaction theory, which assumes that the waves and the turbulence are coupled through the wave-turbulence interactions. Still other models are based on a combination of these theories, or on entirely different approaches.

##### The Future Directions for Research on Internal Wave Breaking Modeling

Despite the significant progress that has been made in the modeling of internal wave breaking, there are still many challenges to overcome. Future research on internal wave breaking modeling should focus on improving the accuracy and reliability of the models, and on developing new models that can capture the complex dynamics of internal wave breaking in different oceanic environments. It should also aim to incorporate more detailed information about the waves and the background flow, and to develop models that can be used to study the effects of internal wave breaking on a wider range of oceanic processes.

#### 3.4d Internal Wave Breaking Mixing

The mixing that occurs as a result of internal wave breaking is a critical aspect of ocean dynamics. It plays a significant role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties, and in the overall circulation of the ocean. In this section, we will discuss the mechanisms of internal wave breaking mixing and its implications for ocean circulation.

##### The Mechanisms of Internal Wave Breaking Mixing

The mixing that occurs as a result of internal wave breaking is primarily due to the generation of turbulence. As internal waves break, they generate turbulence that mixes the water column. This mixing is particularly effective in regions of strong vertical stratification, such as the ocean's thermocline, where the density gradients are large. The turbulence generated by internal wave breaking can also interact with other processes, such as wind mixing and tidal mixing, to further enhance the mixing of the ocean.

##### The Role of Internal Wave Breaking Mixing in Ocean Circulation

The mixing that occurs as a result of internal wave breaking plays a crucial role in ocean circulation. It helps to redistribute heat, nutrients, and other properties throughout the ocean, and to maintain the ocean's stratification. This mixing is particularly important in regions of strong vertical stratification, where it can significantly influence the vertical distribution of properties in the ocean. It can also affect the generation of internal tides, which play a key role in the transport of heat and nutrients in the ocean.

##### The Impact of Internal Wave Breaking Mixing on Climate

The mixing that occurs as a result of internal wave breaking can also have implications for climate. It can affect the distribution of heat in the ocean, and can influence the ocean's response to changes in atmospheric forcing. For example, the mixing of heat by internal wave breaking can help to moderate the ocean's response to changes in surface temperature, and can help to maintain the ocean's thermal structure. It can also affect the ocean's carbon cycle, by influencing the distribution of carbon in the ocean and by affecting the ocean's ability to absorb carbon from the atmosphere.

##### The Future Directions for Research on Internal Wave Breaking Mixing

Despite the significant progress that has been made in understanding internal wave breaking mixing, there are still many unanswered questions. Future research on internal wave breaking mixing should focus on improving our understanding of the mechanisms of internal wave breaking mixing, and on developing more accurate models of internal wave breaking mixing. It should also aim to understand the role of internal wave breaking mixing in climate change, and to develop strategies for mitigating the impacts of climate change on ocean circulation and mixing.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of internal gravity waves, a critical component of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. We have explored the dynamics of these waves, their generation, propagation, and dissipation. We have also examined their role in the transport of energy, momentum, and heat in the atmosphere and ocean. 

We have learned that internal gravity waves are a significant source of mixing and turbulence in the atmosphere and ocean, playing a crucial role in the vertical transport of properties. They are generated by buoyancy fluctuations, which can be caused by various factors such as wind stress, topography, and density stratification. 

The propagation of internal gravity waves is governed by the buoyancy frequency, which is a measure of the stratification of the medium. The waves can travel vertically or horizontally, depending on the direction of the buoyancy frequency. The dissipation of these waves is primarily due to non-linear effects and friction.

In conclusion, understanding internal gravity waves is essential for comprehending the dynamics of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. They play a vital role in the transport of properties and the generation of turbulence, which is crucial for the mixing of the atmosphere and ocean.

## Chapter 4: Atmospheric Boundary Layer

### Introduction

The atmospheric boundary layer (ABL) is a critical component of the Earth's atmosphere, serving as the interface between the overlying atmosphere and the underlying surface. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of the ABL, exploring its dynamics, structure, and role in atmospheric and oceanic circulations.

The ABL is a complex system, influenced by a multitude of factors including solar radiation, wind speed, and surface properties. It is within this layer that many of the most significant atmospheric processes occur, such as the exchange of heat, moisture, and momentum between the atmosphere and the surface. Understanding the ABL is therefore crucial for predicting weather patterns, climate change, and a host of other atmospheric phenomena.

In this chapter, we will explore the ABL from a micro-scale perspective, focusing on the interactions between the atmosphere and the surface. We will examine the physical processes that drive the ABL, such as turbulence and advection, and the mathematical models that describe these processes. We will also discuss the role of the ABL in larger-scale atmospheric and oceanic circulations, and how changes in the ABL can impact these circulations.

Through this exploration, we aim to provide a comprehensive understanding of the ABL, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools to study and analyze this complex system. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply someone with a keen interest in atmospheric science, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of the atmospheric boundary layer and its role in the Earth's atmosphere.




#### 3.4b Internal Wave Mixing Theories

The breaking of internal waves is a crucial process in the mixing of the ocean, and it plays a significant role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and other properties. In this section, we will discuss the various theories that have been proposed to explain the mixing caused by internal wave breaking.

##### The Non-Linear Theory of Motion

The non-linear theory of motion is one of the earliest and most influential theories of internal wave mixing. This theory was first proposed by Miles (1957) and has been further developed by many other researchers. According to this theory, the mixing caused by internal wave breaking is a result of the non-linear interactions between the waves and the background flow.

The non-linear theory of motion is based on the assumption that the waves are weak and that the background flow is dominant. This assumption allows us to neglect the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, resulting in a linear system of equations. However, the linear theory is unable to explain the mixing caused by internal wave breaking, as it predicts that the waves will propagate without changing their form.

To overcome this limitation, Miles (1957) introduced the concept of wave steepening, which is the process by which the waves become steeper as they propagate. This concept is based on the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, which become significant when the waves are steep. According to this theory, the waves become so steep that they break when they reach the critical steepness, resulting in the mixing of the ocean.

##### The Wave-Wave Interaction Theory

The wave-wave interaction theory is another influential theory of internal wave mixing. This theory was first proposed by Keller (1956) and has been further developed by many other researchers. According to this theory, the mixing caused by internal wave breaking is a result of the interactions between different internal waves.

The wave-wave interaction theory is based on the assumption that internal waves can interact with each other, resulting in the mixing of the ocean. This theory explains the mixing caused by internal wave breaking as a result of these interactions. However, like the non-linear theory of motion, the wave-wave interaction theory also relies on the assumption of weak waves and is unable to explain the mixing caused by large internal waves.

##### The Internal Wave Breaking Theory

The internal wave breaking theory is a more recent theory of internal wave mixing. This theory was first proposed by Munk (1966) and has been further developed by many other researchers. According to this theory, the mixing caused by internal wave breaking is a result of the breaking of the waves themselves.

The internal wave breaking theory is based on the assumption that internal waves can break when they reach the critical steepness. This theory explains the mixing caused by internal wave breaking as a result of the turbulence generated by the breaking of the waves. Unlike the other theories, the internal wave breaking theory does not rely on the assumption of weak waves and can explain the mixing caused by large internal waves.

In conclusion, the mixing caused by internal wave breaking is a complex phenomenon that has been studied extensively in the field of oceanography. The non-linear theory of motion, the wave-wave interaction theory, and the internal wave breaking theory are some of the theories that have been proposed to explain this phenomenon. Each of these theories has its strengths and limitations, and further research is needed to fully understand the dynamics of internal wave mixing.




#### 3.4c Internal Wave Breaking and Mixing Models

The study of internal wave breaking and mixing has been a subject of interest for many researchers, and various models have been developed to explain the phenomenon. In this section, we will discuss some of the most influential models of internal wave breaking and mixing.

##### The Wave-Wave Interaction Model

The wave-wave interaction model is a non-linear model that describes the interaction between internal waves. This model is based on the assumption that the waves are weak and that the background flow is dominant. However, unlike the linear theory, this model takes into account the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, which become significant when the waves are steep.

The wave-wave interaction model is based on the concept of wave steepening, which is the process by which the waves become steeper as they propagate. According to this model, the waves become so steep that they break when they reach the critical steepness, resulting in the mixing of the ocean.

##### The Non-Linear Theory of Motion

The non-linear theory of motion is another non-linear model that describes the mixing caused by internal wave breaking. This theory is based on the assumption that the waves are weak and that the background flow is dominant. However, unlike the linear theory, this model takes into account the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, which become significant when the waves are steep.

The non-linear theory of motion is based on the concept of wave steepening, which is the process by which the waves become steeper as they propagate. According to this theory, the waves become so steep that they break when they reach the critical steepness, resulting in the mixing of the ocean.

##### The Wave-Topography Interaction Model

The wave-topography interaction model is a non-linear model that describes the interaction between internal waves and the topography of the ocean floor. This model is based on the assumption that the waves are weak and that the background flow is dominant. However, unlike the linear theory, this model takes into account the non-linear terms in the equations of motion, which become significant when the waves are steep.

The wave-topography interaction model is based on the concept of wave breaking, which is the process by which the waves break when they encounter the topography of the ocean floor. According to this model, the waves break when they reach the critical steepness, resulting in the mixing of the ocean.

##### The Wave-Wave Interaction and Topography Model

The wave-wave interaction and topography model is a hybrid model that combines the wave-wave interaction model and the wave-topography interaction model. This model takes into account both the interactions between internal waves and the topography of the ocean floor.

The wave-wave interaction and topography model is based on the concept of wave breaking, which is the process by which the waves break when they encounter the topography of the ocean floor. According to this model, the waves break when they reach the critical steepness, resulting in the mixing of the ocean.

#### 3.4d Internal Wave Breaking and Mixing Observations

The study of internal wave breaking and mixing has been a subject of interest for many researchers, and various models have been developed to explain the phenomenon. However, it is equally important to observe and study the actual breaking and mixing processes in the ocean. In this section, we will discuss some of the most significant observations of internal wave breaking and mixing.

##### The Role of Topography

One of the most significant factors influencing internal wave breaking and mixing is the topography of the ocean floor. As mentioned in the previous section, the wave-topography interaction model describes the interaction between internal waves and the topography of the ocean floor. This model predicts that the waves break when they reach the critical steepness, resulting in the mixing of the ocean.

Observations have shown that the topography of the ocean floor plays a crucial role in the breaking and mixing of internal waves. For instance, the study by Wunsch and Ferrari (2004) found that the topography of the ocean floor near the Hawaiian Islands plays a significant role in the mixing of the ocean. The study found that the topography of the ocean floor near the islands is complex and irregular, which leads to the breaking of internal waves and the mixing of the ocean.

##### The Role of Wind

Another important factor influencing internal wave breaking and mixing is wind. Wind can generate internal waves, which can then break and mix the ocean. The study by Wunsch and Ferrari (2004) found that the wind field plays a crucial role in the mixing of the ocean. The study found that the wind field near the Hawaiian Islands is strong and variable, which leads to the generation of internal waves and their subsequent breaking and mixing.

##### The Role of Tides

Tides also play a significant role in internal wave breaking and mixing. Tides can generate internal waves, which can then break and mix the ocean. The study by Wunsch and Ferrari (2004) found that the tides near the Hawaiian Islands are strong and variable, which leads to the generation of internal waves and their subsequent breaking and mixing.

##### The Role of Ocean Currents

Ocean currents also play a crucial role in internal wave breaking and mixing. Ocean currents can transport internal waves, which can then break and mix the ocean. The study by Wunsch and Ferrari (2004) found that the ocean currents near the Hawaiian Islands are strong and variable, which leads to the transport of internal waves and their subsequent breaking and mixing.

In conclusion, the study of internal wave breaking and mixing is a complex and ongoing field of research. Observations have shown that various factors, including topography, wind, tides, and ocean currents, play a crucial role in the breaking and mixing of internal waves. Further research is needed to fully understand these processes and their implications for the dynamics and phenomena of the ocean.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of internal gravity waves, a critical component of atmospheric and oceanic circulations. We have explored the dynamics of these waves, their generation, propagation, and dissipation. We have also examined the role of internal gravity waves in the transfer of energy and momentum within the atmosphere and the ocean.

We have learned that internal gravity waves are a significant source of turbulence in the atmosphere and the ocean, playing a crucial role in the mixing of fluids and the redistribution of heat. We have also discovered that these waves are responsible for the vertical transport of energy and momentum, influencing the dynamics of large-scale circulations.

Furthermore, we have discussed the various methods used to study internal gravity waves, including laboratory experiments, numerical simulations, and field observations. These methods have provided valuable insights into the behavior of internal gravity waves, enhancing our understanding of atmospheric and oceanic circulations.

In conclusion, internal gravity waves are a fundamental aspect of atmospheric and oceanic circulations, playing a vital role in the dynamics of these systems. Their study is crucial for understanding the complex interactions between the atmosphere and the ocean, and for predicting future changes in these systems under the influence of climate change.

## Chapter 4: Oceanic and Atmospheric Boundary Layers

### Introduction

The oceanic and atmospheric boundary layers are the interfaces between the ocean and the atmosphere, where the exchange of heat, moisture, and momentum occurs. These layers play a crucial role in the global climate system, influencing weather patterns, ocean currents, and the distribution of marine life. In this chapter, we will delve into the dynamics and phenomena of these boundary layers, exploring their structure, processes, and interactions.

The oceanic boundary layer is the uppermost layer of the ocean, typically extending to a depth of about 20 to 40 meters. It is characterized by turbulent mixing, which is driven by wind and tidal forces. This mixing plays a vital role in the vertical distribution of heat, nutrients, and pollutants in the ocean. We will explore the dynamics of the oceanic boundary layer, including the generation of turbulence, the vertical profiles of temperature and salinity, and the role of internal waves.

Above the oceanic boundary layer is the atmospheric boundary layer, which is the lowest part of the atmosphere. This layer is typically a few hundred meters thick and is influenced by the ocean through processes such as evaporation and condensation. The atmospheric boundary layer is where most of the exchange of heat and moisture between the ocean and the atmosphere occurs. We will examine the dynamics of the atmospheric boundary layer, including the generation of turbulence, the vertical profiles of temperature and humidity, and the role of atmospheric boundary layer clouds.

The study of the oceanic and atmospheric boundary layers is a complex and interdisciplinary field, involving aspects of fluid dynamics, thermodynamics, and atmospheric science. In this chapter, we will provide a comprehensive overview of these topics, drawing on the latest research and observations from around the world. We will also discuss the implications of these boundary layers for climate change and the health of our oceans.




#### 3.4d Internal Wave Breaking and Mixing Experiments

The study of internal wave breaking and mixing has been a subject of interest for many researchers, and various experiments have been conducted to investigate this phenomenon. In this section, we will discuss some of the most influential experiments of internal wave breaking and mixing.

##### The MIT Experiment

The MIT experiment, conducted by researchers at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, was one of the first experiments to study internal wave breaking and mixing. The experiment involved creating a controlled environment in a laboratory setting, where internal waves were generated and propagated in a stratified fluid. The experiment allowed researchers to study the wave breaking and mixing process in a controlled environment, providing valuable insights into the dynamics of internal waves.

##### The RAPID Program Experiment

The RAPID Program experiment, conducted by researchers at the University of Southampton, was a large-scale experiment designed to study the dynamics of internal waves in the ocean. The experiment involved deploying a series of instruments, including acoustic Doppler current profilers (ADCPs) and CTDs, in the North Atlantic Ocean. The data collected by these instruments were used to study the propagation and breaking of internal waves, providing valuable insights into the mixing process.

##### The MOCHA Experiment

The MOCHA (Mediterranean Outflow and Heatflux Array) experiment, conducted by researchers at the University of Miami, was a large-scale experiment designed to study the dynamics of internal waves in the Mediterranean Sea. The experiment involved deploying a series of instruments, including ADCPs and CTDs, in the Mediterranean Sea. The data collected by these instruments were used to study the propagation and breaking of internal waves, providing valuable insights into the mixing process.

##### The RAPID-MOCHA Experiment

The RAPID-MOCHA experiment, conducted by researchers at the University of Southampton and the University of Miami, was a joint experiment designed to study the dynamics of internal waves in the North Atlantic Ocean and the Mediterranean Sea. The experiment involved deploying a series of instruments, including ADCPs and CTDs, in both regions. The data collected by these instruments were used to study the propagation and breaking of internal waves, providing valuable insights into the mixing process.

These experiments have provided valuable insights into the dynamics of internal waves and their role in mixing the ocean. They have also highlighted the importance of considering the effects of topography and wave-wave interactions in models of internal wave breaking and mixing.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of internal gravity waves and their role in the dynamics of the atmosphere and oceans. We have learned that these waves are a result of the interaction between different layers of fluid with different densities, and that they play a crucial role in the transport of energy and momentum within these systems.

We have also delved into the mathematical models that describe these waves, including the dispersion relation and the wave equation. These models have allowed us to understand the behavior of internal gravity waves under different conditions, and to predict their propagation and dissipation.

Furthermore, we have discussed the various phenomena associated with internal gravity waves, such as the generation of turbulence and the mixing of different layers of fluid. These phenomena have important implications for the overall dynamics of the atmosphere and oceans, and for the distribution of heat and nutrients within these systems.

In conclusion, the study of internal gravity waves is a rich and complex field that offers many opportunities for further research and exploration. By understanding these waves, we can gain a deeper understanding of the dynamics of the atmosphere and oceans, and contribute to our understanding of these vital systems.
